<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A chromosome-scale reference genome of Lobularia maritima , an ornamental plant with high stress tolerance"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-12-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Lobularia maritima (L.) Desv. is an ornamental plant cultivated across the world. It belongs to the family Brassicaceae and can tolerate dry, poor and contaminated habitats. Here, we present a chromosome-scale, high-quality genome assembly of L. maritima based on integrated approaches combining Illumina short reads and Hi&#8211;C chromosome conformation data. The genome was assembled into 12 pseudochromosomes with a 197.70&#8201;Mb length, and it includes 25,813 protein-coding genes. Approximately 41.94% of the genome consists of repetitive sequences, with abundant long terminal repeat transposable elements. Comparative genomic analysis confirmed that L. maritima underwent a species-specific whole-genome duplication (WGD) event ~22.99 million years ago. We identified ~1900 species-specific genes, 25 expanded gene families, and 50 positively selected genes in L. maritima. Functional annotations of these genes indicated that they are mainly related to stress tolerance. These results provide new insights into the stress tolerance of L. maritima, and this genomic resource will be valuable for further genetic improvement of this important ornamental plant."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Lobularia maritima</i> (L.) Desv. is an ornamental plant cultivated across the world. It belongs to the family Brassicaceae and can tolerate dry, poor and contaminated habitats. Here, we present a chromosome-scale, high-quality genome assembly of <i>L. maritima</i> based on integrated approaches combining Illumina short reads and Hi–C chromosome conformation data. The genome was assembled into 12 pseudochromosomes with a 197.70 Mb length, and it includes 25,813 protein-coding genes. Approximately 41.94% of the genome consists of repetitive sequences, with abundant long terminal repeat transposable elements. Comparative genomic analysis confirmed that <i>L. maritima</i> underwent a species-specific whole-genome duplication (WGD) event ~22.99 million years ago. We identified ~1900 species-specific genes, 25 expanded gene families, and 50 positively selected genes in <i>L. maritima</i>. Functional annotations of these genes indicated that they are mainly related to stress tolerance. These results provide new insights into the stress tolerance of <i>L. maritima</i>, and this genomic resource will be valuable for further genetic improvement of this important ornamental plant.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD), or polyploidy, has had a strong influence on the evolution of the tree of life, and it seems to have occurred in the evolutionary history of most plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schranz, M. E. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Independent ancient polyploidy events in the sister families Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae. Plant Cell. 18, 1152–1165 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d51408e498">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Van de Peer, Y., Mizrachi, E. &amp; Marchal, K. The evolutionary significance of polyploidy. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 411–424 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d51408e501">2</a></sup>, especially in angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adams, K. L. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Polyploidy and genome evolution in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 8, 135–141 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d51408e505">3</a></sup>. WGDs have been found in most angiosperm families with abundant species, including Brassicaceae, Poaceae, Asteraceae, Solanaceae, Fabaceae and Orchidaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell. 16, 1667–1678 (2004)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d51408e509">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903–9908 (2004)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d51408e509_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. An analysis of synteny of Arachis with Lotus and Medicago sheds new light on the structure, stability and evolution of legume genomes. BMC Genomics. 10, 45 (2009)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d51408e509_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 472–477 (2010)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d51408e509_3">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, Y., Li, J., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses reveal an ancient genome duplication in monocots. Plant Cell. 26, 2792–2802 (2014)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d51408e509_4">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d51408e509_5">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65 (2015)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d51408e509_6">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Huang, C. et al. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 394–412 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d51408e512">11</a></sup>. Previous studies suggested that WGDs can strengthen the adaptation of plants to environmental challenges<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hegarty, M. J. &amp; Hiscock, S. J. Genomic clues to the evolutionary success of polyploid plants. Curr. Biol. 18, R435–R444 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d51408e516">12</a></sup> because of genomic reorganization and novelties. Through subfunctionalization or reciprocal loss of duplicated genes in differentiated populations of an ancestral species, WGDs can also promote reproductive isolation and thus facilitate speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Sémon, M. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Reciprocal gene loss between Tetraodon and zebrafish after whole genome duplication in their ancestor. Trends Genet. 23, 108–112 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d51408e520">13</a></sup>. Brassicaceae (also known as Cruciferae), a monophyletic group distributed worldwide, has been highly diversified by complicated WGD events and subsequent evolution, with ~350 genera and 4000 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Perumal, S. et al. Elucidating the major hidden genomic components of the A, C, and AC genomes and their influence on Brassica evolution. Sci. Rep. 7, 1–12 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d51408e525">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kiefer, M. et al. BrassiBase: introduction to a novel knowledge database on Brassicaceae evolution. Plant Cell Physiol. 55, e3 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d51408e528">15</a></sup>. It contains many important crops (e.g., cabbage, rapeseed and mustard) that have been domesticated for food, biofuels, and ornamentals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Appel, O. &amp; Al-Shehbaz, I. A. Cruciferae. in Flowering Plants·Dicotyledons (Springer, 2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d51408e532">16</a></sup>. The well-known model organism <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, which is of paramount importance in studies of the development, gene expression and genome evolution of flowering plants, is also a member of this family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Al-Shehbaz, I. A., Beilstein, M. A. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Systematics and phylogeny of the Brassicaceae (Cruciferae): an overview. Plant Syst. Evol. 259, 89–120 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d51408e539">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="O’Kane Jr, S. L. Brassicaceae, molecular systematics and evolution of. Brenner’s Encycl. Genet. Second Ed. 374–376, &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/B978-0-12-374984-0.00169-8&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d51408e542">18</a></sup>. Analyses of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome have provided clear evidence that three ancient WGD events (γ, β and α), occurred in its evolutionary history. The oldest WGD event, the At-γ event, was related to the diversification of eudicots and perhaps all angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Blanc, G., Hokamp, K. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. A recent polyploidy superimposed on older large-scale duplications in the Arabidopsis genome. Genome Res. 13, 137–144 (2003)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d51408e549">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="De Bodt, S., Maere, S. &amp; de Peer, Y. Genome duplication and the origin of angiosperms. Trends Ecol. Evol. 20, 591–597 (2005)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d51408e549_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Soltis, D. E. et al. Polyploidy and angiosperm diversification. Am. J. Bot. 96, 336–348 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d51408e552">21</a></sup>. The At-β event postdated the Brassicaceae–Caricaceae divergence ~70 million years ago (Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991–996 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d51408e557">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d51408e560">23</a></sup>. However, the At-α event was specific to the Brassicaceae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Blanc, G., Hokamp, K. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. A recent polyploidy superimposed on older large-scale duplications in the Arabidopsis genome. Genome Res. 13, 137–144 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d51408e564">19</a></sup>, occurring ~40 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Fawcett, J. A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737–5742 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d51408e568">24</a></sup>. In addition, independent WGDs more recent than the Neogene may have promoted the colonization of harsh environments by Brassicaceae taxa by increasing their stress tolerance and conferring high adaptability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d51408e572">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kagale, S. et al. Polyploid Evolution of the Brassicaceae during the Cenozoic Era. Plant Cell. 26, 2777–2791 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d51408e575">26</a></sup>. However, detailed investigation of WGDs in numerous genera present in arid habitats is still badly needed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Guo, X. et al. The genomes of two Eutrema species provide insight into plant adaptation to high altitudes. DNA Res. 25, 307–315 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d51408e579">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Kang, M. et al. A chromosome-scale genome assembly of Isatis indigotica, an important medicinal plant used in traditional Chinese medicine. Hortic. Res. 7, 1–10 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51408e582">28</a></sup>.</p><p><i>Lobularia maritima</i> (L.) Desv., commonly known as sweet alyssum, is a perennial and diploid (2n = 24) herbaceous plant of the family Brassicaceae. This ornamental plant naturally occurs in the western Mediterranean region and has been widely cultivated since its domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang, R. et al. Artificially induced polyploidization in Lobularia maritima (L.) Desv. and its effect on morphological traits. HortScience 50, 636–639 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d51408e591">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Gómez, J. M. et al. Phenotypic selection and response to selection in Lobularia maritima: importance of direct and correlational components of natural selection. J. Evol. Biol. 13, 689–699 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d51408e594">30</a></sup>. Its flowers range in color from pale violet to deep purple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Polunin, O. &amp; Everard, B. Flowers of Europe: A Field Guide (Oxford University Press, Oxford, 1969)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d51408e598">31</a></sup>. In addition to tolerating dry and poor habitats, <i>L. maritima</i> is recognized as a nickel hyperaccumulator that can remove heavy metals from contaminated soils<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yuan, X. Y., Zhang, X. Y., Ma, J. &amp; Hou, X. F. Tissue culture in vitro and establishment of regeneration system of Lobularia maritima. North. Hort. 8, 145–146 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d51408e605">32</a></sup>. As a facultative halophyte closely related to <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>L. maritima</i> seems to be an ideal model for revealing the molecular mechanisms underlying plant tolerance to drought and salt stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Popova, O. V. &amp; Golldack, D. In the halotolerant Lobularia maritima (Brassicaceae) salt adaptation correlates with activation of the vacuolar H+-ATPase and the vacuolar Na+/H+ antiporter. J. Plant Physiol. 164, 1278–1288 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d51408e616">33</a></sup>. However, studies of <i>L. maritima</i> have focused mainly on its cultivation, management and rapid propagation in vitro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang, R. et al. Artificially induced polyploidization in Lobularia maritima (L.) Desv. and its effect on morphological traits. HortScience 50, 636–639 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d51408e623">29</a></sup>.</p><p>In this study, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of the <i>L. maritima</i> genome anchored on 12 pseudochromosomes. We further identified a recent <i>L. maritima</i>-specific WGD event that occurred after the Brassicaceae-specific At-α event using comparative and evolutionary analyses. We also revealed numerous genomic changes by which <i>L. maritima</i> has adapted to harsh habitats.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Samples for genome sequencing were obtained from an <i>L. maritima</i> seedling with purple flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig1">1a</a>). We obtained 59.77 Gb of clean reads with various insert sizes and 22.31 Gb of Hi–C clean reads (~112.89-fold coverage) after Illumina sequencing and quality control (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">1</a>). Two methods were employed to estimate the genome size of <i>L. maritima</i>. First, we determined the <i>L. maritima</i> genome size to be 225 Mb using flow cytometry with <i>A. thaliana</i> as the external control (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM1">1</a>). Second, we used <i>k</i>-mer-based statistics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Liu, B. et al. Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects. Quant. Biol. 35, 62–67 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d51408e676">34</a></sup>, and the genome size was calculated to be 264 Mb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Morphological and genomic characteristics of L. maritima."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Morphological and genomic characteristics of <i>L. maritima</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="617" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Morphological characteristics of <i>L. maritima</i>: schematic representations of (a) aerial parts; (b) abaxial surfaces of leaves; (c) adaxial surfaces of leaves; (d) flowers; (e) petals; and (f) a receptacle and pedicel. <b>b</b> Hi–C chromatin interaction map for the 12 pseudochromosomes of the <i>L. maritima</i> genome. <b>c</b> Genome comparison between <i>L. maritima</i> and <i>C. rubella</i> at the chromosome level: (a) syntenic relationships between the <i>L. maritima</i> and <i>C. rubella</i> genomes; (b) gene density (window size = 100 kb, nonoverlapping); (c) density distribution of <i>Copia</i> elements (window size = 100 kb, nonoverlapping); and (d) density distribution of <i>Gypsy</i> elements (window size = 100 kb, nonoverlapping). <b>d</b> Estimated insertion times of intact LTR retrotransposons</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Based on the clean reads, a de novo genome was assembled with a 197.70 Mb length. We further anchored this genome on 12 pseudochromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig1">1b</a> and Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM1">3</a>). We then evaluated the completeness of this genome using BUSCO v4.1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d51408e755">35</a></sup> and found that 99% of the single-copy orthologs were intact (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">2</a>), suggesting the high quality of the assembled genome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation statistics of the <i>L. maritima</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>To predict protein-coding sequences, we combined de novo and homology- and transcriptome-based methods. We predicted 25,813 complete protein-coding genes. Gene length and the number of exons of these protein-coding genes were 2431 base pairs (bp) and 5.46 exons, respectively, on average (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Tab1">1</a>). In our assembly, 97.99% (25,295 of 25,813) of the genes were annotated on 12 pseudochromosomes, and only 2.01% (518 of 25,813) were located on scaffolds. The Circos v0.69 (<a href="http://circos.ca">http://circos.ca</a>) was used to visualize the collinearity blocks between <i>L. maritima</i> and <i>Capsella rubella</i>, gene density, <i>Copia</i> density, and <i>Gypsy</i> density on individual chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig1">1c</a>). Among the 25,813 predicted genes, 81.30% and 95.71% had homologs in the Swiss-Prot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1023">36</a></sup> and TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1027">36</a></sup> databases, respectively. Additionally, we annotated 95.15%, 80.65%, and 36.55% of the genes using the InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211–D215 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1031">37</a></sup>, Gene Ontology (GO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1035">38</a></sup> and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: Kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1040">39</a></sup> databases, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">3</a>). In addition, 41.94% (83 Mb) of the assembled <i>L. maritima</i> genome comprised repetitive sequences (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">4</a>). Of these repetitive sequences, long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were the most frequent, spanning 14.24% of the assembled genome with 13.23% intact LTR retrotransposons. The other common repetitive sequences were DNA transposons (10.06%), Tandem Repeats (8.56%) and LINEs (5.65%) (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">5</a>). To analyze the evolutionary dynamics of these LTRs, we estimated their insertion dates in four related species (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i>, <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>L. maritima</i>). The recent insertions in <i>A. lyrata</i> may have contributed to its relatively large genome size (207 Mb). Similarly, <i>L. maritima</i> had more recent insertions than <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>C. rubella</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">5</a>). Diverse genetic changes can be caused by transposable elements (including LTR retrotransposons), which might have promoted lineage-specific diversification and adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Oliver, K. R., McComb, J. A. &amp; Greene, W. K. Transposable elements: powerful contributors to angiosperm evolution and diversity. Genome Biol. Evol. 5, 1886–1901 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1091">40</a></sup>. This may partly contribute to the tolerance of <i>L. maritima</i> to arid habitats. However, the <i>L. maritima</i> genome contained a similar number of transcription factors (1799) as the other closely related Brassicaceae species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">6</a>, all transcription factor data for other species were downloaded from <a href="http://www.transcriptionfactor.org">http://www.transcriptionfactor.org</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparative genomic analyses and WGD analyses</h3><p>Using the ColinearScan v1.0.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wang, X. et al. Statistical inference of chromosomal homology based on gene colinearity and applications to Arabidopsis and rice. BMC Bioinforma. 7, 447 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1120">41</a></sup> program and MCScanX v1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1124">42</a></sup> package, the protein sequences of <i>L. maritima</i> were compared to those of the diploid <i>C. rubella</i>, which has not been affected by a recent WGD event, to identify the collinear blocks in the genomes. The whole-genome alignments showed high collinearity and conservation, and several collinear regions almost completely spanned chromosomes of the two species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig2">2</a>). It is worth noting that each chromosome or chromosomal region in <i>C. rubella</i> was represented on multiple independent chromosomes in the <i>L. maritima</i> genome after the Brassicaceae-specific At-α WGD event, suggesting that the <i>L. maritima</i> genome experienced a specific WGD event.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Dotplot comparing the L. maritima and C. rubella genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Dotplot comparing the <i>L. maritima</i> and <i>C. rubella</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="595" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Collinear regions in the <i>L. maritima</i> genome. Regions from putative subgenomes are circled in purple and green, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Furthermore, we determined the karyotype of <i>L. maritima</i> using previously reported methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Kang, M. et al. A chromosome-scale genome assembly of Isatis indigotica, an important medicinal plant used in traditional Chinese medicine. Hortic. Res. 7, 1–10 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1183">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Lysak, M. A., Mandáková, T. &amp; Schranz, M. E. Comparative paleogenomics of crucifers: ancestral genomic blocks revisited. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 108–115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1186">43</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM1">4</a>) and recovered two sets of conserved genomic blocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Mandáková, T., Guo, X., Özüdoğru, B., Mummenhoff, K. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Hybridization-facilitated genome merger and repeated chromosome fusion after 8 million years. Plant J. 96, 748–760 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1193">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Mandáková, T. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Chromosomal phylogeny and karyotype evolution in x = 7 crucifer species (Brassicaceae). Plant Cell. 20, 2559–2570 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1196">45</a></sup>. However, the patterns of genomic blocks suggested that <i>L. maritima</i> experienced many postpolyploid diploidization events and a reduction in chromosome number. We also analyzed the gene retention rates of the two subgenomes in each genomic block with the <i>C. rubella</i> genome as the reference. The results showed that the two subgenomes retained similar numbers of genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">7</a>). We also assessed the absence or presence of genome dominance by examining the expression levels of each pair of duplicated genes with high confidence. Based on RNA-seq data from flower, leaf, and stem tissues, we failed to find any evidence of biased expression in each genomic block between the two subgenomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">8</a>). These results are largely consistent with the patterns of autopolyploids, which usually show a few instances of biased gene retention and no genome dominance.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Recent WGD event in <i>L. maritima</i>
</h3><p>To identify possible WGD events, we calculated the Ks values between the collinear genes. The <i>L. maritima</i> collinear blocks produced two visible peaks, at 0.583 and 1.287 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig3">3a</a>), representing two different WGD events. We then estimated the occurrence times of each WGD event based on the Ks values. However, dating ancestral events in plants can be influenced by divergent evolutionary rates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, J. et al. Two likely auto-tetraploidization events shaped kiwifruit genome and contributed to establishment of the Actinidiaceae family. iScience 7, 230–240 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1231">46</a></sup>. Thus, by aligning the <i>L. maritima</i> peak with the corresponding location in the <i>C. rubella</i> Ks distribution, as in a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, J. et al. Two likely auto-tetraploidization events shaped kiwifruit genome and contributed to establishment of the Actinidiaceae family. iScience 7, 230–240 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1242">46</a></sup>, we performed evolutionary rate correction (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig3">3b</a>). After correction, the peaks of Ks for the two WGD events were 0.378 and 0.855, corresponding to 22.99 and 52.01 Mya, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig3">3b</a>). The results indicated an ancient WGD event shared with <i>C. rubella</i> and a recent species-specific WGD event in <i>L. maritima</i>. In addition, Ks estimation indicated that <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>L. maritima</i> diverged approximately 21.53 Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig3">3b</a>). These findings were consistent with those of the synteny and collinearity analyses of <i>L. maritima</i> and <i>C. rubella</i> and suggested that <i>L. maritima</i> experienced a species-specific WGD event after sharing a WGD event with other Brassicaceae species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Evolutionary rate correction."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Evolutionary rate correction.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="411" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of uncorrected Ks values in syntenic blocks; <b>b</b> distribution of corrected Ks values in syntenic blocks and age estimates for the events</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Phylogeny and divergence</h3><p>We obtained the genome sequences of representative Brassicaceae species to clarify the genome evolution and divergence of <i>L. maritima</i>. Gene family clusters were defined based on the <i>L. maritima</i> protein-coding genes and the annotated gene sets of 10 published genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">9</a>) using OrthoFinder v2.3.12<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1320">47</a></sup>. A total of 25,316 orthogroups were determined across the 11 species. Among these orthogroups, 1,986 were putative single-copy gene families, and 24,705 genes from <i>L. maritima</i> could be clustered into 16,821 orthogroups. In addition, we identified 1878 <i>L. maritima</i>-specific genes in these gene families. Functional annotations of these genes indicated that they were distinctly enriched in the GO terms “positive regulation of response to salt stress”, “abscisic acid-activated signaling pathway”, “response to freezing”, “response to stimulus”, and “response to biotic stimulus”, indicating that the genes retained after the WGD event may be relevant in the adaptation of <i>L. maritima</i> to multiple environmental stress factors (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig4">4a</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM3">11</a>). For example, a homolog of these <i>L. maritima</i>-specific genes, <i>ABI4</i>, acts as both an activator and a repressor of gene expression and plays a critical role in phytohormone signaling pathways in plant development and biotic/abiotic stress responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Chandrasekaran, U., Luo, X., Zhou, W. &amp; Shu, K. Multifaceted signaling networks mediated by abscisic acid insensitive 4. Plant Commun. 1, 100040 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1350">48</a></sup>. Another homolog, <i>ABI1</i>, serves as a key repressor of the abscisic acid (ABA) signaling pathway and regulates diverse ABA responses to abiotic stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Harb, A., Krishnan, A., Ambavaram, M. M. R. &amp; Pereira, A. Molecular and physiological aanalysis of drought stress in Arabidopsis reveals early responses leading to acclimation in plant growth. Plant Physiol. 154, 1254–1271 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1357">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Kariola, T. et al. Early responsive to dehydration 15, a negative regulator of abscisic acid responses in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 142, 1559–1573 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1360">50</a></sup>. The species-specific calcium-dependent protein kinase (<i>CDPK</i>) genes recovered here (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM3">11</a>) were also demonstrated to be involved in numerous aspects of plant growth and development, from sensing biotic and abiotic stress to mediating hormone-related development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Boudsocq, M. &amp; Sheen, J. CDPKs in immune and stress signaling. Trends Plant Sci. 18, 30–40 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1371">51</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Evolutionary analyses of the L. maritima genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Evolutionary analyses of the <i>L. maritima</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="591" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Gene Ontology enrichment of species-specific genes in <i>L. maritima</i> compared to 10 other species. <b>b</b> Estimation of divergence times between 11 species in the Brassicaceae family. The <i>A. thaliana</i> α and β duplication events were estimated to have occurred ~50 and ~60 million years ago, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1402">76</a></sup>. <b>c</b> Phylogenetic tree of the <i>KTI</i> gene family constructed from sequences of <i>L. maritima</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, and <i>S. parvula</i>, indicating the expanded gene copies in <i>L. maritima</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To verify the phylogenetic position of <i>L. maritima</i>, we used the concatenated protein sequence alignment of the 1986 single-copy gene families in the 11-species phylogenetic analyses. The results confirmed that <i>L. maritima</i> belonged to Lineage II<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Huang, C. et al. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 394–412 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1441">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Beilstein, M. A., Al-Shehbaz, I. A. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Brassicaceae phylogeny and trichome evolution. Am. J. Bot. 93, 607–619 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1444">52</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig4">4b</a>), consistent with its position in the chloroplast genome phylogeny reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Guo, X. et al. Plastome phylogeny and early diversification of Brassicaceae. BMC Genomics. 18, 176 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1451">53</a></sup>. In our analyses performed using MCMCtree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1456">54</a></sup>, <i>L. maritima</i> was estimated to have diverged from the other closely related species ~22.63 (18.74, 26.61) Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig4">4b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Expansion and contraction of gene families in <i>L. maritima</i>
</h3><p>Gene families with significantly expanded or contracted copy numbers are usually related to the adaptive divergence of one species from closely related species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913–918 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1478">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Sudmant, P. H. et al. Diversity of human copy number variation and multicopy genes. Science 330, 641–646 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1481">56</a></sup>. We compared the genomes of <i>L. maritima</i> and 10 other species, with <i>Aethionema arabicum</i> as the outgroup (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig4">4b</a>), to explore the expansion and contraction of the gene families in <i>L. maritima</i>. Twenty-five gene families, comprising 319 genes, were significantly expanded in <i>L. maritima</i> (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05). Functional annotation of these genes indicated that they were mainly enriched in “response to molecule of bacterial origin”, “response to insect”, “response to molecule of fungal origin”, “response to wounding” and “response to salt stress” (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">13</a>). For example, one of the expanded gene families, the <i>KTI</i> gene family, comprised versatile protease inhibitors related to defense against insect attack (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig4">4c</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Arnaiz, A. et al. Arabidopsis kunitz trypsin inhibitors in defense against spider mites. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 986 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1517">57</a></sup>. In addition, the <i>HIPP</i> gene family, involved in stress responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Zschiesche, W. et al. The zinc-binding nuclear protein HIPP3 acts as an upstream regulator of the salicylate-dependent plant immunity pathway and of flowering time in Arabidopsis thaliana. N. Phytol. 207, 1084–1096 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1524">58</a></sup>, was also greatly expanded in <i>L. maritima</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Positively selected genes in <i>L. maritima</i>
</h3><p>Genes with signs of positive selection are usually regarded to be involved in the adaptive divergence of one species from closely related species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Fitch, W. M. Distinguishing homologous from analogous proteins. Syst. Zool. 19, 99–113 (1970)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1543">59</a></sup>. We conducted positive selection analysis by using <i>L. maritima</i> as the foreground branch and five related Brassicaceae species (<i>Eutrema yunnanense</i>, <i>C. rubella</i>, <i>A. arabicum</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i>, and <i>Schrenkiella parvula</i>) as the background branches. We identified 10,581 single-copy orthologous gene families. To identify the genes that evolved in response to positive selection, we adopted the branch-site model in the PAML v4.9 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1566">54</a></sup>. After false discovery rate (FDR) correction, we identified 50 genes that were possibly under positive selection. The functions of the significantly positively selected genes (PSGs) indicated that they were associated with stress tolerance and the survival of plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#Fig5">5</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#MOESM2">14</a>). For example, one of the genes was <i>SGT1B</i>, which was found to be involved in innate immunity and resistance in plants mediated by multiple R genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Azevedo, C. et al. Role of SGT1 in resistance protein accumulation in plant immunity. EMBO J. 25, 2007–2016 (2006)." href="#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1580">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tör, M. et al. Arabidopsis SGT1b is required for defense signaling conferred by several downy mildew resistance genes. Plant Cell. 14, 993–1003 (2002)." href="#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1580_1">61</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Holt, B. F. Antagonistic control of disease resistance protein stability in the plant immune system. Science 309, 929–932 (2005)." href="#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1580_2">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Austin, M. J. Regulatory role of SGT1 in early R gene-mediated plant defenses. Science 295, 2077–2080 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1583">63</a></sup>. Another of the genes was <i>YchF1</i>, which is involved in salinity stress tolerance and disease resistance against bacterial pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Cheung, M.-Y. et al. ATP binding by the P-loop NTPase OsYchF1 (an unconventional G protein) contributes to biotic but not abiotic stress responses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 2648–2653 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1590">64</a></sup>. Another of the genes, <i>EIF4A3</i>, is an important factor for abiotic stress adaptation, which can regulate plant resistance to abiotic stress partially by regulating the expression of acetoacetyl-CoA thiolase 2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Pascuan, C., Frare, R., Alleva, K., Ayub, N. D. &amp; Soto, G. mRNA biogenesis-related helicase eIF4AIII from Arabidopsis thaliana is an important factor for abiotic stress adaptation. Plant Cell Rep. 35, 1205–1208 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1597">65</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Genes in the L. maritima genome associated with various biotic and abiotic stimuli."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Genes in the <i>L. maritima</i> genome associated with various biotic and abiotic stimuli.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00422-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_422_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="505" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Gene IDs for the gene names are listed below. <i>ABI1</i>: Lma13276; <i>ABI4</i>: Lma14310, Lma25376; <i>EFR</i>: Lma05693, Lma14720, Lma21740; <i>EIF4A3</i>: Lma15462; <i>ERD15</i>: Lma14373, Lma26266; <i>ERF012</i>: Lma17999; <i>HIPP06</i>: Lma19275; <i>HIPP14</i>: Lma12975; <i>HIPP33</i>: Lma25212; <i>HIPP43</i>: Lma16268; <i>KTI1</i>: Lma22625, Lma04403, Lma25677, Lma22622, Lma22621, Lma04406, Lma22624, Lma22623, Lma25676, Lma04405; <i>MYB96</i>: Lma13875; <i>STG1B</i>: Lma21436; <i>PME1</i>: Lma13269; <i>ROSY1</i>:Lma11854; <i>RPP13L4</i>: Lma09152, Lma03536, Lma21662, Lma03543, Lma09149, Lma21664, Lma09145; <i>RTM3</i>: Lma20357, Lma22531, Lma20352, Lma10871, Lma08006, Lma20353; <i>TIL</i>: Lma24858, Lma24859; <i>DSC2</i>: Lma14716 Lma17306; <i>YchF1</i>: Lma17801; Abbreviations: ABI ABA insensitive; EFR EF-TU receptor; EIF eukaryotic initiation factor; ERD early responsive to dehydration stress; ERF ethylene-responsive factor; HIPP heavy metal-associated isoprenylated plant protein; KTI Kunitz trypsin inhibitor; MYB myeloblastosis oncogene; PME pectin methyl esterase; ROSY interactor of synaptotagmin; RPP resistance to <i>P. pachyrhizi</i>; RTM restricted tobacco etch virus movement; TIL temperature-induced lipocalin; DSC2, desmocollin-2</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p><i>L. maritima</i> is an important ornamental plant in horticulture because of its colorful flowers and stress tolerance. In this study, by combining Illumina and Hi–C data, a chromosome-level high-quality <i>L. maritima</i> genome was assembled. The <i>L. maritima</i> genome was ~197.70 Mb in size, and 88.31% (174.59 Mb) of the sequences were assigned to 12 pseudochromosomes. We annotated 25,813 genes and found substantially more repetitive elements (especially intact LTR retrotransposons) in the <i>L. maritima</i> genome than in the genomes of other Brassicaceae species. In addition, most intact LTR retrotransposons expanded rapidly in the recent past. Such proliferation of LTR retrotransposons may have partly resulted in the increased genome size of <i>L. maritima</i>. Phylogenetic reconstructions showed that <i>L. maritima</i> diverged early as an independent branch of Brassicaceae Lineage II.</p><p>In the histories of many diverse eukaryotes, including <i>Danio rerio</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Postlethwait, J. H. et al. Zebrafish comparative genomics and the origins of vertebrate chromosomes. Genome Res. 10, 1890–1902 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1722">66</a></sup>, <i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Wolfe, K. H. &amp; Shields, D. C. Molecular evidence for an ancient duplication of the entire yeast genome. Nature 387, 708 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1728">67</a></sup>, and <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Blanc, G., Barakat, A., Guyot, R., Cooke, R. &amp; Delseny, M. Extensive duplication and reshuffling in the Arabidopsis genome. Plant Cell. 12, 1093–1101 (2000)." href="#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1734">68</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vision, T. J., Brown, D. G. &amp; Tanksley, S. D. The origins of genomic duplications in Arabidopsis. Science 290, 2114–2117 (2000)." href="#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1734_1">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Simillion, C., Vandepoele, K., Van Montagu, M. C. E., Zabeau, M. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. The hidden duplication past of Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 99, 13627–13632 (2002)." href="#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1734_2">70</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Bowers, J. E., Chapman, B. A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433–438 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1737">71</a></sup>, WGDs have been discovered. Through large-scale phylogenomic analyses, ancient WGDs were found to occur in the common ancestors of both seed plants and angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell. 16, 1667–1678 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1741">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1744">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Bowers, J. E., Chapman, B. A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433–438 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1747">71</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Doyle, J. J. et al. Evolutionary genetics of genome merger and doubling in plants. Annu. Rev. Genet. 42, 443–461 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1750">72</a></sup>. WGDs have played an essential role in angiosperm diversification and environmental adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1755">9</a></sup>. Polyploids can tolerate high environmental stress, with present-day polyploids often appearing to occur at high frequencies in disturbed and harsh environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Madlung, A. Polyploidy and its effect on evolutionary success: old questions revisited with new tools. Heredity 110, 99 (2013)." href="#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1759">73</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ramsey, J. Polyploidy and ecological adaptation in wild yarrow. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 7096–7101 (2011)." href="#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1759_1">74</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Diallo, A. M., Nielsen, L. R., Kjær, E. D., Petersen, K. K. &amp; Ræbild, A. Polyploidy can confer superiority to West African Acacia senegal (L.) Willd. trees. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 821 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1762">75</a></sup>. Under environmental stresses, polyploids may have been more successful because their changing environments created many opportunities to make use of the evolutionary benefits of WGDs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1766">76</a></sup>. The comparison of <i>L. maritima</i> and the diploid <i>C. rubella</i> indicated a recent WGD event that was specific to <i>L. maritima</i>, followed by extensive chromosomal rearrangements. Furthermore, we evaluated whether biased gene retention occurred after the WGD event. Two subgenomes retained a similar number of genes. However, neither subgenome showed genome dominance. This indicates that <i>L. maritima</i> might have undergone an autopolyploidization event. Analysis of the Ks values between the collinear genes suggested that the recent <i>L. maritima</i>-specific WGD event occurred ~22.99 Mya. The comparison of between-species Ks distributions indicated that the <i>L. maritima</i>-<i>C. rubella</i> divergence occurred ~21.53 Mya. Thus, this divergence and the aforementioned <i>L. maritima</i>-specific WGD event occurred at almost the same time. <i>L. maritima</i> and <i>C. rubella</i> belong to two major lineages, and it is highly likely that the divergence of the two major lineages and genus diversification of each lineage in Brassicaceae occurred radiatively at the same time. This rapid radiation was accompanied by polyploidy in a few of the genera. This is also consistent with the previous suggestion that further WGDs might have occurred in Brassicaceae since the Neogene, with radiative diversification, which further helped members of this family colonize arid habitats by increasing their stress tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kagale, S. et al. Polyploid Evolution of the Brassicaceae during the Cenozoic Era. Plant Cell. 26, 2777–2791 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1802">26</a></sup>. As a result of the WGD event, species-specific genes and expanded gene families become further involved in responses to environmental stresses, for example, drought and pathogen attack, which might have facilitated the adaptation of <i>L</i>. maritima to harsh environments. In addition, the positively selected genes in <i>L. maritima</i> may have increased defense against fungal and bacterial attack. Thus, the species-specific WGD event may have promoted the adaptation of <i>L. maritima</i> to harsh environments, which is consistent with previous findings for numerous plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1815">76</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Vanneste, K., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Tangled up in two: a burst of genome duplications at the end of the Cretaceous and the consequences for plant evolution. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. B Biol. Sci. 369, 20130353 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1818">77</a></sup>. These genomic traits may also explain why <i>L. maritima</i> is a nickel hyperaccumulator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yuan, X. Y., Zhang, X. Y., Ma, J. &amp; Hou, X. F. Tissue culture in vitro and establishment of regeneration system of Lobularia maritima. North. Hort. 8, 145–146 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1826">32</a></sup> and a halophyte with a high tolerance to salt stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Popova, O. V. &amp; Golldack, D. In the halotolerant Lobularia maritima (Brassicaceae) salt adaptation correlates with activation of the vacuolar H+-ATPase and the vacuolar Na+/H+ antiporter. J. Plant Physiol. 164, 1278–1288 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1830">33</a></sup>. Overall, whole-genome sequencing of <i>L. maritima</i> could elucidate the stress tolerance of this ornamental plant and be useful in future breeding programs.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Materials and DNA/RNA extraction</h3><p>The <i>L. maritima</i> seedling was cultivated in Jinjiang District, Chengdu City, Sichuan Province, China (N 30°34′21.86″, E 104°09′45.47″). We harvested fresh and healthy roots, stems, leaves and flowers and immediately froze them in liquid nitrogen. Before DNA/RNA extraction, we stored these tissues in a −80 °C freezer in the laboratory. To extract high-quality genomic DNA, the cetyl trimethylammonium bromide (CTAB)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Doyle, J. L. A rapid DNA isolation procedure for small quantities of fresh leaf tissue. Phytochem. Bull. 19, 11–15 (1987)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00422-w#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d51408e1852">78</a></sup> method was used. Additionally, we extracted total RNA from the flower, stem and leaf tissues using Qiagen RNeasy Plant Mini Kits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>We randomly fragmented the purified genomic DNA using a focused ultrasonicator and obtained fragments of desired lengths by electrophoresing the DNA fragments in 0.8% General Purpose Agarose E-Gel. Then, we created Illumina libraries with large (2-, 5-, 10- and 20-kb) and small (350- and 500-bp) inserts using the purified DNA fragments. Based on the PE-150 protocol, the libraries were finally sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. RNA libraries were constructed with a TruSeq RNA Library Preparation Kit v2 and sequenced on the same platform.</p><p>A Hi–C library was constructed using five main steps. First, we fixed the sample with formaldehyde and crosslinked DNA-DNA interactions that are bridged by proteins. Second, the crosslinked DNA was treated with the restriction endonuclease Hind III to produce sticky ends. Third, terminal DNA repair was used to introduce biotin-labeled bases in order to facilitate subsequent DNA purification and capture. Next, we ensured the location of the interacting DNA through cyclization of the end-repaired DNA and DNA fragments. Finally, we extracted and purified the DNA sample and then used Covaris S2 to shear the DNA sample. After A-tailing, pulldown, and adapter ligation, the DNA library was sequenced on an Illumina platform using the PE-150 protocol. We used HiCPro v2.8.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Servant, N. et al.
